        <p class="date">
         Sat Mar 12, 2011 at 04:08 PM PST
        </p>
        <h2>
         <a href="/story/2011/03/13/955752/-The-State-of-Public-Education-from-a-Student-s-Perspective-My-First-Diary" id="titleHref">
          The State of Public Education from a Student's Perspective (My First Diary)
         </a>
         <span style="white-space: nowrap">
         </span>
        </h2>
        <p class="author">
         <span>
          by
         </span>
         <a href="/user/thePhoenix13">
          thePhoenix13
         </a>
         <a alt="Follow this author" class="ajax type-user do-hotlist do-updateContent size-tiny off altTooltip" href="#?friend_id=296520&amp;is_stream=1">
          Follow
         </a>
        </p>
        <ul class="front-page-share clearfix">
         <li style="margin-left: 0px">
          <div class="fb-share-button" data-href="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2011/03/13/955752/-The-State-of-Public-Education-from-a-Student-s-Perspective-My-First-Diary" data-layout="button_count">
          </div>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="twitter-share-button" data-count="horizontal" data-related="markos" data-text="The State of Public Education from a Student's Perspective (My First Diary)" data-url="http://www.dailykos.com/story/2011/03/13/955752/-The-State-of-Public-Education-from-a-Student-s-Perspective-My-First-Diary" data-via="dailykos" href="https://twitter.com/share">
           Tweet
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="comments comment_count_with_new" data-comment_count="120" data-story_id="955752" href="/story/2011/03/13/955752/-The-State-of-Public-Education-from-a-Student-s-Perspective-My-First-Diary#comments">
           120 Comments
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </div>
       <div class="article-body">
        <div id="intro">
         <p>
          I graduated from a relatively large public high school in a impoverished area in rural Maine last year, having completed all 12 years in the local public school system. In case you havent noticed, there has been a lot of discussion on this site since the attacks on teachers unions. As someone who has experienced first hand the effects of No Child Left Behind and the budget shortfalls at the federal, state, and local level, I feel like I should share my experience. There have been quite a few diaries posted here by teachers and parents, but I havent seen any by students.
         </p>
         <p>
          Before I begin to talk about everything that is terribly wrong with the public school system in this country, let me just say that Im not doing it because it ended badly for me. I couldnt be in a better place, and Im happy to have spent all my years in the schools and surrounded by the people that I was. However, it worked out so well for me largely because I had a solid family situation, and I was self-motivated enough to accomplish what I needed to accomplish. However I did stand witness to all of those kids that it didnt work out so well for, and it was obvious that things were only getting worse as I left.
         </p>
         <p>
          So, let us begin.
         </p>
        </div>
        <p class="divider-doodle">
        </p>
        <div class="article-body" id="body">
         <p>
          First of all, the impact of the man, the myth, the legend, Mr. George Walker Bush and his No Child Left Behind. This introduced a whole new era of public education, and its not a pretty one. As Im sure youre all aware, this, in a nutshell, was intended to increase the education of all students by requiring them to meet certain standards of education. Those schools that didnt measure up would be held accountable for this. In theory, it really doesnt sound so bad; it seems reasonable to make sure that kids are actually learning things at school.
         </p>
         <p>
          But it was bad; it was really bad. Somewhere between the forming the concept and enacting the law, something went horribly, horribly wrong. Instead of simply keeping tabs on students progress over the years, it turned into a way of forcing students to only try and be average. Adding to that, someone thought it made sense to cut funding from under-performing schools as punishment for not living up to the national standards, which of course only made things worse, trapping some schools in a steady decline that was only compounded by the recession.
         </p>
         <p>
          For instance, everyone in my school had to take the SAT in junior year; apparently the state said that it could be used in place of some other state standardized test we wouldve had to take otherwise. The school made a very huge deal about this. We had assemblies about it, just about every relevant class would do some test prep, there were study sessions offered after school for months beforehand, and the school basically did everything they could to make sure we knew this was a BIG DEAL. It turns out my school did really well, much to my surprise, considering I knew many people who either slept most of the time or just went C, C, C, C, C, etc. But, apparently all that extra work paid off, so crisis averted, we didnt lose even more funding. It makes me wonder though, what happened to the school in last place? Where one too many kid made cool patterns in the bubbles? Obama hadnt instituted his Meet The Standard Or We Burn Your School To The Ground policy, so at least the school was probably still there. But now, this school that obviously needs a way to motivate and educate their kids, which can really only be done with extra funding, is going to have to cut programs and positions, and likely offer less support to the kids. Whose idea was this again? Sure, the fear of not passing motivated my school enough to get enough of the kids into gear and meet the standard, but now those who dont cut it are basically just left for dead.
         </p>
         <p>
          The result of this loss of fundings means that schools start losing vital things. These schools end up with larger classes, and cant level kids as well anymore. Since the only motivation was to make everyone meet the standard  there was no real reward for having some people exceed  then everyone just gets mushed together into the same classes to try and make everyone pass.
         </p>
         <p>
          They started to do this at my school too because they found some grant that required following this system, but fortunately I just barely escaped this part of the mess. As I moved into my junior year they decided to stop leveling certain freshman and sophomore classes. While mathematics may say this simply means everyone just comes closer to the former median in education, reality says that it just brings everyone way down. The smart kids completely lose motivation because they can do everything with incredible ease and are just bored out of their minds all day, and the kids who struggle with the class feel beat down because they see how easy it is for some of the kids and so they lose motivation too. It just isnt possible for everyone to get mixed together, and then BAM everyone can meet the standard. A chain is only as strong as its weakest link, and in public school, chances are youll have a very weak link in just about every class. The reason we have public school is so that everyone can achieve some level of education, but its ridiculous to try and make everyone reach the same level.
         </p>
         <p>
          Then, of course, there was the financial crisis. Any school that had already been having budget issues because of NCLB was surely done for now, and my school  which had so complacently succeeded at the SATs  had already been struggling with money for awhile, so it became even worse. My school decided to implement a budget freeze in order to try and save jobs. Besides paper, classes couldnt buy anything. I was in Advanced Placement chemistry last year, a class where you take a test at the end of the year for college credit. Fortunately, a lot of the chemicals were already stockpiled enough for our class, but still, by the end of the year there were some experiments that we just had to skip because the school couldnt buy more chemicals. My teacher is also supposed to get rid of chemicals that have been around too long, since, according to state safety people, they might have been unsafe to use. So my teacher was supposed to get rid of some of the chemicals he had, and he wasnt allowed to buy more. That is just a crappy situation to be in. I can only imagine what that class is doing this year. This was a similar story for every class in the school that required any resources at all, which was basically all of them.
         </p>
         <p>
          But somehow, even though we had resorted to a complete budget freeze, in the past couple years no one got laid off. Teachers offered to take furlough days instead of people losing their jobs. The school also did not fill most positions left vacant by people leaving the school. Chemistry classes didnt buy chemicals. Great, I thought, we can all make some sacrifice so people can keep their jobs. But it is so sad for me to realize that this was a victory. Our school had accomplished what so many public schools, especially in impoverished areas, had failed to do: not lay anyone off. Our public school system has come to the point where the best anyone can hope for is not that class sizes will decline, not that more people will get into ivy leagues, not that more classes can be offered to give students a more varied education, but that no one gets fired this year.
         </p>
         <p>
          It is shocking how far the system has fallen. Thirty years ago it wasnt uncommon for people from my school to get into Harvard and Stanford and the like. But in the four years I was there I saw two people get into ivy leagues, one who spent the first three years of high school at a math and science charter school, and one who was homeschooled most of his life. Ninety percent of kids in the U.S. go to public school, yet only about sixty percent of kids at Stanford went to public high school. Thats a pretty huge difference, and unfortunately its somewhat common with Ivy League type schools. But these are students who are trying to learn while surrounded by the chaos of deficits and cuts, and are probably being taught by a teacher fighting to keep their job.
         </p>
         <p>
          Fiscal conservatism has basically turned the public school system into Lord of the Flies, and something huge needs to happen to fix it. The first huge thing of course would be to tax the rich so hard it makes their head spin and speed up that goddamn trickling down. But its going to be a very, very long time before that happens  or any positive reform it seems  so all we can do is just hope the kids still going through the system make it out alive.
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>
       <h4>
        Originally posted to
        <a href="/blog/thePhoenix13/">
         thePhoenix13
        </a>
        on Sat Mar 12, 2011 at 04:08 PM PST.
       </h4>

        <br/>
        <div>
         <p>
          Add keywords that describe this diary.
		Separate multiple keywords with commas.
          <br/>
          <a href="/special/tag_tips" target="_blank">
           Tagging tips
          </a>
          -
          <a href="/search?search_tags=1" target="_blank">
           Search For Tags
          </a>
          -
          <a href="http://www.dkosopedia.com/wiki/Tag:Tagfinder" target="_blank">
           Browse For Tags
          </a>
         </p>
         <div class="pageTip hasTooltip" style="margin-top:3px">
          ?
         </div>
         <div class="tooltipContents qtip-style-dk4_large qtip-pos-br">
          <p>
           <b>
            More Tagging tips:
           </b>
          </p>
          <p>
           A tag is a way to search for this diary. If someone is searching for "Barack Obama," is this a diary they'd be trying to find?
          </p>
          <p>
           Use a person's full name, without any title. Senator Obama may become President Obama, and Michelle Obama might run for office.
          </p>
          <p>
           If your diary covers an election or elected official, use election tags, which are generally the state abbreviation followed by the office. 
		CA-01 is the first district House seat. CA-Sen covers both senate races. NY-GOV covers the New York governor's race.
          </p>
          <p>
           Tags do not compound: that is, "education reform" is a completely different tag from "education". A tag like "reform" alone is probably not meaningful.
          </p>
         </div>
         <!-- &lt;h4 id="p_tagErrorMessage"&gt;You must enter at least one Tag for this Diary Entry:&lt;/h4&gt; -->
         <p>
          <input id="p_tagInput" name="tag_draft" size="70" type="text" value=""/>
          <!-- &lt;input id="tagSubmit" type="submit" name="submit" value="Submit" /&gt; -->
         </p>
         <p style="font-style:italic">
          Consider if one or more of these tags fits your diary:
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Civil Rights');return false;">
           Civil Rights
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Community');return false;">
           Community
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Congress');return false;">
           Congress
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Culture');return false;">
           Culture
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Economy');return false;">
           Economy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Education');return false;">
           Education
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Elections');return false;">
           Elections
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Energy');return false;">
           Energy
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Environment');return false;">
           Environment
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Health Care');return false;">
           Health Care
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('International');return false;">
           International
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Labor');return false;">
           Labor
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Law');return false;">
           Law
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Media');return false;">
           Media
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Meta');return false;">
           Meta
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('National Security');return false;">
           National Security
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Science');return false;">
           Science
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Transportation');return false;">
           Transportation
          </a>
          , or
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('White House');return false;">
           White House
          </a>
          .
	If your diary is specific to a state, consider adding the state 
	(
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('California');return false;">
           California
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="#" onclick="addTagToField('Texas');return false;">
           Texas
          </a>
          , etc).
	Keep in mind, though, that there are many wonderful and important diaries
	that don't fit in any of these tags. Don't worry if yours doesn't.
         </p>

             <p>
              this impacts the future of our country more than anything else.
             </p>
             <p>
              Without critical thinking skills we are sunk. And test prep is just rote learning, one thing we know is detrimental to any coping much less progress.
             </p>
             <p>
              In the past it has been found that the primary reason for dropping out was boredom. (secondary was need to earn money  often for family needs.)
             </p>
             <p>
              But cutting out all things that are rewarding to students especially those who are not really into academics, like band, art, and all the "enriching" subjects is false economy.
             </p>
             <p>
              You are right, many kids will survive, but too many will be left behind and even damaged by these wrong headed policies.
             </p>
             <p>
              Please continue to think and write about education. You write very well.

           <li id="c28">
            <div class="cx" id="cx40746428">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               There was a school next county over.
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/955752/40746428#c28?mode=alone;showrate=1#c28">
                14+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:73764">
                 philipmerrill
                </a>

                <a href="/user/uid:75462">

                </a>

                <a href="/user/uid:274938">
                 MartyM
                </a>

                <a href="/user/uid:20449">

                </a>

                <a href="/user/uid:73842">

                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:200859">
                 peregrine kate
                </a>

                <a href="/user/uid:114690">

                </a>

                <a href="/user/uid:93519">
                 linkage
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:240319">
                 congenitalefty
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:116838">
                 elwior
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:24892">
                 samddobermann
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               Students dwindling in this one old school. They were going to consolidate it with this other HS about 10 or less miles away.
              </p>
              <p>
               Parents in town mobilized the county and got a levy passed and did a new school.
              </p>
              <p>
               Small rural area, fairly republican but honestly I think the main reason they passed it was because they didn't want to lose their football team.
              </p>
             </div>

              <p class="sig">

           <li id="c87">
            <div class="cx" id="cx40751542">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               That's nothing new
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/955752/40751542#c87?mode=alone;showrate=1#c87">
                2+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:116838">
                 elwior
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:24892">
                 samddobermann
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               When I was in high school, the #1 priority was the football team. They had the special gym, lots of money was lavished on the school band and the cheerleader uniforms. Nothing else mattered. When there were school board elections, the sole issue was having a winning football team. Nobody cared about anything else.
              </p>
              <p>
               The steel mills died, people lost their jobs, the economy collapsed, but the school board still cared about the football team. The football coach demanded a larger salary and more perks, he got them, and everything else was cut back. Football is their religion, it's driving them to economic death, they don't care.
              </p>
              <p>
               I can tell you from my experience at Pitt that it was only worse, Football was everything. And it's gotten worse since I graduated 35 years ago...
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
              </p>
              <p class="cb">

        <li id="c23">
         <div class="cx" id="cx40745691">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Where public school students come from...
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/955752/40745691#c23?mode=alone;showrate=1#c23">
             12+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:221952">
              Shakespeares Sister
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:15174">
              elfling
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:75462">
              Translator
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:45368">
              historys mysteries
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:13069">
              Stein
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:73842">
              4Freedom
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:200859">
              peregrine kate
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:114690">
              mamamedusa
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:164745">
              consciousempress
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:116838">
              elwior
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:24892">
              samddobermann
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:154707">
              Dragon5616
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            ...affluent backgrounds, they do quite well, usually because their parents pony up to pay the taxes for quality schools and a quality education. The students in areas of high poverty rates (where parents have nothing to pony up) tend to do poorly. Approximately 20% of US students come from families living below the poverty level. In contrast, is Finland, second-ranked in reading and first overall in the OECD PISA tests, only 3% live in poverty.
           </p>
           <p>
            Reading scores from Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) PISA scores (2010) with US results also broken out by percent of school population qualifying for free or reduced-price lunch program (FRPL):
           </p>
           <table border="1">
            <tr>
             <td>
              % of Students Eligible for Free/Reduced-Price Lunches vs. OECD benchmarks and US average
             </td>
             <td>
              Score

            <p class="cb">

              Translator
             </a>

          <li id="c93">
           <div class="cx" id="cx40752807">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              Doc:  It's HTML table code
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/955752/40752807#c93?mode=alone;showrate=1#c93">
               0+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              Or at least my poor attempts at it. There are a number of good tutorials online that you should be able to find in a Google search, and there's a bit in the FAQ as well.
             </p>
             <p>
              The basic code is &lt;table&gt; to start the command, and &lt;/table&gt; to end it. In between will be table rows ( &lt;tr&gt; to start and &lt;/tr&gt; to end. ) within which is the table data ( &lt;td&gt; to start and &lt;/td&gt; to end. ).
             </p>
             <p>
              There are also a number of parameters that can be used in the table command itself or in the table data commands. The only one I used was a border parameter, border="1".
             </p>
             <p>
              It would look something like this:
              <br/>
              &lt;table border="1"&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;tr&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;td&gt; row 1&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 1 &lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 2 &lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 3 &lt;/td&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;/tr&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;tr&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;td&gt; row 2&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 4 &lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 5 &lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 6 &lt;/td&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;/tr&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;tr&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;td&gt; row 3 &lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 7 &lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 8 &lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt; 9 &lt;/td&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;/tr&gt;
              <br/>
              &lt;/table&gt;
             </p>
             <p>
              Which ought to give you something like this (which for some reason is showing up at the very bottom of the comment in preview instead of immediately after this line which is where I have it in the composition window) --
             </p>
             The hardest part is getting everything typed accurately. It's easy to get confused where you are in the table and type a td when it should be tr, or forget one, or use an opening command where you should have had a closing, and when your eyes are as bad as mine, those mistakes are damned hard to spot.
             <p>
              Where I used to use tables a lot was to caption photos. I'd put the img src="image file name" inside a table with the text all in the same data command, and if I constrained the width of the table with a width parameter ( width="300" or width
              <code>
               "300px" or width
              </code>
              "33%" or something like that, the caption would word-wrap within the width of the table and appear as a caption on the image. You can see it at work in many of my pre-DK4 diaries. Right now, though, the table command is automatically filling the entire column and ignoring width commands, so this technique doesn't work very well anymore. (Elfling is probably getting tired of hearing me bitch about this...)
             </p>
             <table border="1">
              <tr>
               <td>
                row 1
               </td>
               <td>
                1
               </td>
               <td>
                2
               </td>
               <td>
                3
               </td>
              </tr>
              <tr>
               <td>
                row 2
               </td>
               <td>
                4
               </td>
               <td>
                5
               </td>
               <td>
                6
               </td>
              </tr>
              <tr>
               <td>
                row 3
               </td>
               <td>
                7
               </td>
               <td>
                8
               </td>
               <td>
                9
               </td>
              </tr>
             </table>
            </div>

             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">
               We can have democracy in this country, or we can have great wealth concentrated in the hands of a few, but we can't have both. - Justice Louis D. Brandeis
              </p>
              <p class="cb">
               by
               <a href="/user/uid:61397">
                dsteffen
               </a>
               on
               <a href="/comments/955752/40753094#c95">
                Sun Mar 13, 2011 at 12:47:04 PM PDT
               </a>

               <a href="#c93">

               </a>

              </p>
             </div>
            </div>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </ul>
         <li id="c65">
          <div class="cx" id="cx40750264">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             It's easier to teach in wealthier areas..
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/955752/40750264#c65?mode=alone;showrate=1#c65">
              13+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:114690">
               mamamedusa
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:13069">
               Stein
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:179177">
               ladywithafan
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:221952">
               Shakespeares Sister
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:151063">
               NWTerriD
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:80807">
               shaharazade
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:157079">
               not this time
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:116838">
               elwior
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:63966">
               CJnyc
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:61397">
               dsteffen
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:164832">
               CrissieP
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:24892">
               samddobermann
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:154707">
               Dragon5616
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             I currently teach in a poverty-level Title I school, but have also taught in middle-to-upper class area schools.
            </p>
            <p>
             It is much more difficult to teach in poor areas. Poor families can't afford computers and basic school supplies, causing kids to miss valuable learning opportunities at home. The parents are often working two jobs each, so the kids have to fend for themselves (and often watch younger siblings) at night, typically in over-crowded apartments. As such, they struggle to get homework done. Also, many don't have cars, which means taking their child to the library or coming to a school event is a huge time investment. Earlier this week, I had a student come in tired as she had to accompany her dad as they rode three buses the night before to drop-off their rent check.
            </p>
            <p>
             When I taught in wealthier areas, the teachers received needed classroom materials donated by parents. The school boosters were even able to retain, through fundraising, some music and special science programs when budgets were cut. I could assign homework, including editing papers or research to be saved on an external drive, and know the work would be done. Parents in such schools are able to regularly volunteer in classrooms and were an important part of supporting school events.
            </p>
            <p>
             For these many reasons and more (differences in access to health care and adequate food, etc.), students in middle-to-upper class areas can more easily pass the NCLB tests. As such, their teachers can devise more creative lessons, assign more complex readings, and the schools can support having separate classes for higher-level learners.
            </p>
            <p>
             However, those of us who teach in Title I schools are constantly facing scrutiny and high-level pressure to get our students to pass the same state test. Therefore, we have stopped assigning novels because short stories and magazine articles make it easier to teach to the test. We have been removing some higher-level classes as schools are using the smarter students to lift the level of discourse in the regular classrooms. In simpler language: smart kids are being used as peer support to lift the test scores of average-to-low students. And, yes, I have several gifted students who I know would love to be in an advanced class. As a teacher, I have to create extra lessons for those students to keep them from becoming completely bored and disinterested.
            </p>
            <p>
             I predict that the era of bland, flat education will increase as national standards, and a national exam, will soon replace the state tests.
            </p>
            <p>
             So, young diarist, your voice is needed. Please continue to speak out against those who work to remove creativity and joy from U.S. classrooms.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
             We cannot solve the problems that we have created with the same thinking that created them." - Albert Einstein
            </p>

           <li id="c75">
            <div class="cx" id="cx40750551">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               Schools used to provide more...
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/955752/40750551#c75?mode=alone;showrate=1#c75">
                3+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:13069">
                 Stein
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:116838">
                 elwior
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:61397">
                 dsteffen
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               I grew up in California before Prop. 13 (reduction in property taxes) was passed. In that era, the schools gave us our own workbooks, art supplies, and all basic materials were provided in the classroom. There were also several field trips each year. However, when taxes were reduced, the classroom supplies slowly vanished.
              </p>
              <p>
               Now, in middle-class area schools, teachers give students a list of required supplies - and parents jam the local office supply stores each September. However, in poverty-level schools, we can't ask our parents to purchase supplies as they just don't have the means to do so.
              </p>
              <p>
               So, in reducing taxes, particularly on the wealthier Americans, the schools have pushed the burden for providing supplies to the parents - and, if the parents cannot afford supplies, their children sit in our classrooms begging peers to borrow a pencil...
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">

            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Worry that many won't want to teach...
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/955752/40750483#c72?mode=alone;showrate=1#c72">
              7+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:13069">
               Stein
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:236662">
               bbkenn92
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:221952">
               Shakespeares Sister
              </a>

              <a href="/user/uid:151063">
               NWTerriD
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:157079">
               not this time
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:116838">
               elwior
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:169086">
               Funkygal
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             I've heard from bright college students that the attacks on teachers makes them reticent from entering the profession.
            </p>
            <p>
             I wonder if the diatribe against teachers is a means to diminish the importance of being educated and dismantle the structure of our society. For many young people, the only educated people they know are their teachers. The school is, for many kids, a place where they can lift themselves beyond the troubling issues they face at home. It is in the framework of the school where most kids learn to think abstractly and ponder issues by using imagination.
            </p>
            <p>
             By tearing-down teachers, politicians are telling students that teaching is not important and teachers are to no longer be treasured and respected. The emphasis on standardized testing then further removes creativity and independent thinking from U.S. classrooms.
            </p>
            <p>
             Thus, a dismantling of one of our most important societal structures, the public schools, continues...
            </p>

           <li id="c66">
            <div class="cx" id="cx40750265">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               And just as many do not.
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/955752/40750265#c66?mode=alone;showrate=1#c66">
                3+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="crs">
              <dl>
               <dt>
                Recommended by:
               </dt>
               <dd>
                <a href="/user/uid:13069">
                 Stein
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:116838">
                 elwior
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="/user/uid:24892">
                 samddobermann
                </a>
               </dd>
              </dl>
             </div>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               I had a great GPA and was in the Honor Society in HS, but I do not always do well on standardized tests, in part because mild dyscalculia causes numbers to flip or transpose. I'm fine with written tests (essays or written questions) and fine with those that use images or symbols (good visual/spatial sense); it's just the numbers that give me a hard time -- on those bubble tests I will frequently mark the wrong row (9 instead of 6, say) and then most or all the subsequent answers will be wrong. I won't notice it until I look at the page and spot a "hole' (blank area) in the data. So frustrating!
              </p>
              <p>
               They did not make accommodations for people with dyslexia/dyscalculia back when I was in school...hell, I never even figured out what was going on until I was out of school entirely and took some standardized tests at my workplace (a graduate school affiliated with Harvard Med). All of a sudden the fact that I had the high score (among my fellow college-bound classmates) in Verbal, and the low one for Math -- a one-woman Bell Curve -- made sense!
              </p>
              <p>
               I'm not alone. Our class Valedictorian (perfect 4.0 average) tanked on her SATs too. She just didn't test well, and those tests were not an accurate reflection of either her current knowledge or future success.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">

         <li id="c38">
          <div class="cx" id="cx40748771">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Not in the sense you mean
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/955752/40748771#c38?mode=alone;showrate=1#c38">
              6+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:75462">
               Translator
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:88592">
               Guy Fawkes
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:45368">
               historys mysteries
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:13069">
               Stein
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:221952">
               Shakespeares Sister
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:116838">
               elwior
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             Money provides the means to motivate students. Students aren't automatically motivated by the thought of their school having extra money.
            </p>
            <p>
             School funding should depend on what's needed, not on test results. Schools having student populations that need extra help and extra motivation should get more money regardless of the NCLB testing.
            </p>
            <p>
             For example, a school with a largely poor student base which suffers from a lack of support from parents and the community might receive funding to decrease class sizes and provide programs in music, sports, and other extra-curriculars, which keep students involved in school. If the students begin to do better under that system, this should not then be claimed as success and discontinued, but should be used as a model for other schools.
            </p>
            <p>
             It was a mistake to tie funding to school performance like that in the first place, as a reward or a punishment. The criteria for funding levels should be based on need.
            </p>
           </div>

           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Money is a two edged sword
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/955752/40748939#c48?mode=alone;showrate=1#c48">
              6+ / 0-

             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             Your point is certainly well taken. But at the same time, cutting off funding for schools that are underperforming only serves to insure that they will have to do more with less in the future, trapping them in the cycle the diarist described perfectly. It's a horrible catch-22. Give money to schools that are failing and it looks like you are rewarding failure. Take it away and you all but guarantee that the school will remain a failure.
            </p>
            <p>
             The reality is that investments have to be made in some of the heavily impoverished, failing schools in order to get the facilities, equipment and teacher pay up to spec. It will look like rewarding failure, but in reality it is an investment in future success.
            </p>
           </div>

              <a href="/user/uid:24892">
               samddobermann
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             You have just been exceedingly positive in your comments, so I thank you for that. I am fully aware that I owe most of my success so far to my upbringing, and there aren't many people out there who can say otherwise. I'm glad that you are doing the same for your kids, I wish everyone would.
            </p>
            <p>
             I'll be sure to check out your diaries, pimp away!
            </p>
            <p>
             Do you mean you'd like me to post this diary at that site, or are you saying I should just do that in general? Thank you for the invitation either way.
            </p>
            <p>
             I think the split infinitive thing has sort been on the rise ever since "to boldly go where no man has gone before". I can't say I was really around before that, but I'm just assuming that was the start of it's rise in popularity. I honestly don't even notice it when I'm writing, which probably isn't good.
            </p>

          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            You did it! Great first diary.
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            Since the only motivation was to make everyone meet the standard  there was no real reward for having some people exceed
           </blockquote>
           <p>
            To further illustrate your point - recently, in a discussion with my son's teacher, we talked about the balancing act she needs to perform between the high performers, kids struggling and everyone in the middle. In preparation for the upcoming testing season, she said the word had come down to focus on the kids in the middle as they had the greatest chance of improving - and helping the test scores.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">

        <ul class="cm i1" id="k40749396">
         <li id="c63">
          <div class="cx" id="cx40750061">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             I believe that more needs to be done.
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/955752/40750061#c63?mode=alone;showrate=1#c63">
              4+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:13069">
               Stein
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:73842">
               4Freedom
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:70434">
               Catesby
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:40967">
               Aug6PDB
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             In my fifth grade class there were 5 problem kids that always managed to mess things up for everybody else. My teacher believed that they deserved all her attention so she ignored the average kids and smart kids in favor of the problem kids. Even the curriculum for our class was designed specifically with the 5 problem kids in mind. This had negative consequences for me because I fell behind in math compared to my peers in other schools. Up to this point, I had been the star pupil in math growing up, confident in my ability to do math. my previous teachers even had me helping the slower kids with their math problems, which I was happy to do. The 5 problem kids caused so much trouble in class that we did not cover all the material that we were supposed to cover in math.
            </p>
            <p>
             Then, the summer before the start of 6th grade I had to take a math exam to determine what level of math I would take in the 6th grade.  The middle school offered a summer math class to prep for the math exam. Imagine my horror when I begin to take the prep class only to realize that I was far behind my peers in that class. The other kids had gone to good public schools that didn't have 5 problem kids hold back their learning. Needless to say, I struggled in the class and my confidence in math was shot. I did badly on the math exam and ever since have been bad at math. Playing catch up in math + no confidence = disaster. Looking back now, I realize that one bad year in the 5th grade really hurt me. More needs to be done to prevent problem kids from ruining the learning experience for other kids.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
            </p>
            <p class="cb">
             by
             <a href="/user/uid:97309">
              Sarella Sand
             </a>
             on
             <a href="/comments/955752/40750061#c63">
              Sun Mar 13, 2011 at 08:05:04 AM PDT
             </a>
            </p>

             <a href="#c54">

             </a>

              </a>
             </p>
             <p class="cl">
              [
              <a href="#c63">
               Parent
              </a>

          <ul class="cm i1" id="k40753723">
           <li id="c118">
            <div class="cx" id="cx40760272">
             <h5>
              <a class="de">
              </a>
              <span class="cu">
               Not necessarily. I thought that until I
              </span>
              <span class="crd ntb">
               (
               <a href="/comments/955752/40760272#c118?mode=alone;showrate=1#c118">
                0+ / 0-
               </a>
               )
              </span>
             </h5>
             <div class="ct">
              <p>
               walked in to my 6th period science class the first day. They announced to me "We are the dumb kids. We can't learn anything." They weren't so dumb that they couldn't tell that the classes G and H were bottom of the barrel after the A through F groups.
              </p>
              <p>
               That broke my heart, that these kids were so wounded. They knew their label and felt that all they could do would be to live up to that. They also got the oldest, most used, most error filled books while the newest with a lot more pictures and diagrams went to the Highest level. I told them nonsense, they were certainly able to learn, and they would. Moreover they all knew something that no one else knew, we would just have to find out what. During the year I asked them what they thought about things we discussed, what did they know? A lot of them blossomed. Fortunately the administration hoo hahs ignored those classes.
              </p>
              <p>
               Schools kill a lot of kids inside. If you are in the advanced groups you get pampered, almost a private school education. Any trouble makers, any one not swift enough, or doesn't show it right off, will get booted out, err, get transferred to a more suitable class. Just like private schools can dump any "difficult" students, any atypical students, unless atypically brilliant or talented  but sometimes one of those doesn't fit the mold so out he goes. Public schools have to take all the private school rejects.
              </p>
              <p>
               That happened to a friend of mine in high school. He went all the way down to the "vocational level," those who would be fit only for a vocational education. When we were talking and he said he was dumb I said no, he was very smart, very intelligent, I insisted... He asked how did I know, why was I so sure? I said because we were friends, long time friends. If he were dumb he would be boring and I would be able to take it and we wouldn't be friends.
              </p>
              <p>
               He quit school when I left and went in to the Coast Guard. They gave recruits an IQ test. He scored 160, the top. So they gave him an individual test. He scored 158.
              </p>
              <p>
               That's who the schools trashed. He refused Officer's Training and served his 30 years.
              </p>
              <p>
               I have taught both ways, high, mid and low levels and all in one. 6th through 8 grade, Science, Math and even Social studies, private and public schools.
              </p>
              <p>
               By the way, HS Biology was ruined for me by a bad teacher. I even burned my Biology notebooks and stuff, ceremonially at the end of the year. I vowed to avoid it in the future.
              </p>
              <p>
               By chance I happened to take a biology course in my third year and it just turned me on. I went on to get my BS in Biology, with a concentration in Genetics.
              </p>
              <p>
               Don't rule out Biology: it's biophysics, bioengineering, neuroscience, epidemiology (My field for my MPH), ecology, environmental science(s), sustainable agriculture, marine science, and on and on.... this is the Biology Century, like the 20th was the Physics Century.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="cf">
              <p class="sig">

            <li id="c120">
             <div class="cx" id="cx40764955">
              <h5>
               <a class="de">
               </a>
               <span class="cu">
                You bring up a great point.
               </span>
               <span class="crd ntb">
                (
                <a href="/comments/955752/40764955#c120?mode=alone;showrate=1#c120">
                 0+ / 0-
                </a>
                )
               </span>
              </h5>
              <div class="ct">
               <p>
                Leveling the classes isn't the end-all solution, it still requires classes to be set up well. It is very true that a lot of lower level classes end up being neglected, and are usually only saved by a dedicated teacher. My step-mom was one of those teachers who only wanted to work with lower-level kids, and she did end up helping them, and standing up to the administration for them on occasion. It sounds like you may be one of those teachers as well. School administrations are often very quick to label students, and not let them overcome that, no matter how much the administration says they're all about encouraging achievement. One of my friends started out in the highest level classes, but over the course of high school he moved down further and further because he just hated school, it wasn't a good environment for him. He is one of the smartest kids I know, but high school just wasn't for him. I'm not sure of his IQ, but I'm sure it's up there.
               </p>
               <p>
                I think it depends what school you're in, but I know at my school, being in the more advanced classes wasn't exactly being pampered. I took a fair amount of AP classes, and so by senior year I was mostly in classes with kids on a similar level as myself, but it's not like we were completely separated from the kids in other levels. Everything besides english, math, and chemistry was the same for me as everyone else last year. I would say our special treatment by the administration consisted more of being ignored than given special privileges.
               </p>
               <p>
                Anyways, there is no doubt that lower leveled classes end up being deprived of attention. De-leveling classes certainly isn't the answer to these problems, it just creates more, but leveled classes certainly need more work because they do end up treating kids unfairly.
               </p>
               <p>
                That's actually pretty funny, me and a few of my friends did the exact same thing with our bio notebooks. I have enough interests outside of biology, so I think I'll be alright without it. I know there are enough kids who are interested in the subject to take care of it for me.
               </p>
              </div>

-Maurice Sendak

           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:48073">
             lilypew

        <li id="c59">
         <div class="cx" id="cx40749682">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Last place
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/955752/40749682#c59?mode=alone;showrate=1#c59">
             6+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:13069">
              Stein
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:25555">
              oneworld
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:21422">
              sfgb
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:73842">
              4Freedom
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:114690">
              mamamedusa
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:24892">
              samddobermann
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            What happens to the schools in last place? As a teacher in AZ, a state chronically ranked last or next to last in per pupil funding, and on the Navajo reservation as well, I can tell you what happens to last place schools. They get taken over, staff are summarily dismissed and forced to reapply for their position, curriculum is dictated from the capitol in Phoenix, and a monstrous amount of time-consuming documentation is required to be provided. This is very profitable for a few admins and privatized service providers. However, a huge amount of the public school support literally runs out before even getting to the kids. The kids at the end of the lunch lines regularly are facing a bologna sandwich because the day's meal ran out before they got to the window.
           </p>
           <p>
            Much of this is pure politics.
           </p>
           <p>
            In the Navajo Nation, efforts to maintain the Dine' language and cultural teachings, literally decades in the making in public education, are brusquely brushed aside in order to serve the AYP mandates of the mainstream society. The tests are culturally biased, and local control is lost. The harmful English-only laws make things intolerable. After years of gains to regain tribal sovereignty, the Native American people are once again thrust back into colonialism, and their very existence as unique cultures is placed in serious jeopardy. They are being alienated in their own land! Ask any Penobscot person.
           </p>
           <p>
            Much of this is bigotry, pure and simple.
           </p>
           <p>
            But most of it is the mind-numbing indifference of greed-driven power.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            Poverty exists in direct proportion to greed.
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:40585">
             jcrit
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/955752/40749682#c59">
             Sun Mar 13, 2011 at 07:28:21 AM PDT
            </a>
           </p>

           </p>

            Reading this diary of yours tells me you did well for yourself in the school system. I enjoyed your first diary &amp; will read your work to see where you take us.
           </p>
           <p>
            Thanks for sharing
           </p>
          </div>

        <li id="c62">
         <div class="cx" id="cx40749999">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Well - I'll make the same comment here
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/955752/40749999#c62?mode=alone;showrate=1#c62">
             5+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:13069">
              Stein
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:21422">
              sfgb
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:73842">
              4Freedom
             </a>

             <a href="/user/uid:60191">
              Anna M
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:221952">
              Shakespeares Sister
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            that I made in Sumner's front-page diary: The ultimate republican agenda is to dismantle the public school system. There is little reason to believe No Child Left Behind wasn't engineered to create failure.
           </p>
           <p>
            By the way, as a private-school-educated person, I'll also throw out something I learned way back, but didn't hit me till lately: private schools that receive no federal funding aren't affected by NCLB or any other federal mandate for test scores or whatever. We were and still are free from that nonsense. The only standardized tests I've ever taken in 8 years of grade school and four years of high-school are the SAT &amp; ACT.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:168506">
             ban48
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/955752/40749999#c62">
             Sun Mar 13, 2011 at 07:59:25 AM PDT

          </div>

        </li>
        <ul class="cm i1" id="k40749999">
         <li id="c68">
          <div class="cx" id="cx40750378">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             Many private schools in my area do administer
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/955752/40750378#c68?mode=alone;showrate=1#c68">
              2+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:296520">
               thePhoenix13
              </a>
              ,
              <a href="/user/uid:24892">
               samddobermann
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             standardized tests (live in northern NJ). My understanding is that these schools need to be able to see how their students do versus national norms, including affluent suburban communities and other private schools. That said, there is very little teaching to the test in many of these schools and the schools are free to experiment and to constantly develop best practices. In addition, I think that even though private school teachers are, on average, paid less than public school teachers, private school teachers seem to be very happy to be able to concentrate only on instruction and not local politics. Private school teachers also have an advantage because a greater number of parents tend to be engaged since they are paying for the school - and teachers don't have to deal with discipline problems since the school can put these kids out. Public school teachers have to deal with so much more than "taxpayers" will acknowledge. It really baffles me that Americans, even Republicans, don't seem to get this, don't seem to want to provide proper support, and seem to be in favor of failing schools. I just can't understand it. Everyone has a story about a difference that a good teacher made on his/her educational experience. And I don't care what anyone says, merit pay may not have much of an impact once teachers are already in the classroom, but higher compensation levels would definitely attract more individuals into teaching who themselves were high performing students which should translate into more skilled teachers.
            </p>
           </div>

           </p>
           <p class="cb">

        <li id="c76">
         <div class="cx" id="cx40750612">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            I've often wondered if NCLB
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/955752/40750612#c76?mode=alone;showrate=1#c76">
             2+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:221952">
              Shakespeares Sister
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:80807">
              shaharazade
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            originally came from the military (or at least their way of training), which is all about rote memorization and learning things by "task, condition, and standard".  You learn (memorize) the facts, take a test on them, and move on to the next set of facts. Good soldiers don't question or discuss things like morality, outside points of view, or personal feelings about an issue. You don't stop and discuss the moral implications of your actions. Everything is laid out as good or bad, patriot or traitor, us or "them".
           </p>
           <p>
            So now we have NCLB, which has the same basis; learn the basic facts but don't think too much.  Strange, I remember studying the old Soviet Union - wasn't that the way they taught their children in schools - rote memorization and test scores?
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">

        <li id="c77">
         <div class="cx" id="cx40750632">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            student activities
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/955752/40750632#c77?mode=alone;showrate=1#c77">
             1+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:24892">
              samddobermann
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            I think there is probably a direct link between cutting student activities and the lack of motivation by students. I know for me a main driver of doing well in school was to make sure I could be on my cross country &amp; debate teams. While I overall did OK in school anyway making sure I had at least the 3.0 GPA to be on both those teams made a big difference.
           </p>
           <p>
            It also, of course, gave me a lot of confidence &amp; school pride to represent my school in those activities. Without school activities school would probably feel like a prison.
           </p>
           <p>
            Well written diary - please keep spreading this message and keep fighting for the 90% of people in public schools.We need a highly educated youth to lead us into the future!
           </p>
          </div>

  <script type="text/javascript">
   if($.cookie('meter_user') !== 'false') {
		if ($('#ePostButton').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('#ePostButton').click(function() { 
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Posted Comment', { 
		      repliedTo: $(this).parents('#eP').prevAll('.cb').children('a').first().html() 
		    });
		  });
		}
		
		if (!!document.location.pathname.match('activate') &amp;&amp; $('h1:contains("Welcome to Daily Kos!")').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Signed Up');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname === '/') {
		  if (document.location.hostname.match(/^www|^dailykos/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Front Page');
		  } else {
		    var subdomain = document.location.hostname.split('.')[0];
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Vertical', { name: subdomain });
		  }
		}
		
		if ($('.ratinate').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.ratinate').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Comment') });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-addRating.type-story').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-addRating.type-story').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Story') });
		}
		
		if ($("form[action$='publishHow']").length &gt; 0) {
		  $(document).ready(function() {
			  $('.type-story.do-publishHow').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Published Story') });
			 });
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/user/) &amp;&amp; $('li.here').length &gt; 0) {
		  if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Stream/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Stream', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Profile/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Profile', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:last li.here').children('a').html().match(/My Recent Replies/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Replies');
		  }
		} 
		
		if ($('#search-tabbed').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Searched');
		}
		
		if ($('#group-tables').length &gt; 0) {
			DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Groups List');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/groups/) &amp;&amp; $("#group_info").length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Group', { name: $('.name').children('a').text() });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').click(function() {
		    var nickName;
		    nickName = ($(this).prev('a').html() || $('.name a').html())
		    if ($(this).hasClass('do-hotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Followed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    } else if ($(this).hasClass('do-unHotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Unfollowed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    }    
		  });
		}
	}
  </script>
